       This article is about a branch of languages. It is not to be confused with
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German language
       </a>
       .
      </div>

         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Anglo-America" title="Anglo-America">

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Netherlands" title="Caribbean Netherlands">

         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">
          Suriname
         </a>
         ),
         <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">
          Southern Africa
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">
          Oceania
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>

           </span>

            Indo-European studies
           </a>
          </div>

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Indo-European_topics" title="Template:Indo-European topics">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indo-European_topics" title="Template talk:Indo-European topics">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indo-European_topics&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Germanic languages
       </b>
       are a branch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">
        language family
       </a>
       spoken natively by a population of about 515 million people
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [nb 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mainly in
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">
        North America
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">
        Oceania
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">
        Southern Africa
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic languages
       </a>
       include the three most widely spoken Germanic languages:
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       with around 360–400 million native speakers;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NE100_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NE100-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [nb 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       , with over 100 million native speakers;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch
       </a>
       , with 23 million native speakers. Other West Germanic languages include
       <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       , an offshoot of Dutch, with over 7.1 million native speakers;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low German
       </a>
       , considered a separate collection of
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">
        unstandardized
       </a>
       dialects, with roughly 0.3 million native speakers and assuming 6.7—10 million people who can understand it
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gechattet_wird_auf_Plattdeusch_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gechattet_wird_auf_Plattdeusch-8">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TOLoE_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TOLoE-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ndsethnologue_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ndsethnologue-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (5 million in Germany
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gechattet_wird_auf_Plattdeusch_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gechattet_wird_auf_Plattdeusch-8">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 1.7 million in the Netherlands);
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       , once used by approximately 13 million
       <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
        Jews
       </a>
       in pre-
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       Europe
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yivo-yiddish_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-yivo-yiddish-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">
        Scots
       </a>
       , both with 1.5 million native speakers;
       <a href="/wiki/Limburgish_language" title="Limburgish language">
        Limburgish varieties
       </a>
       with roughly 1.3 million speakers along the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Dutch
       </a>
       –
       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
        Belgian
       </a>
       –
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        German
       </a>
       border; and the
       <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">
        Frisian languages
       </a>
       with over 0.5 million native speakers in the Netherlands and Germany.
      </p>
      <p>
       The main
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
       are
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">
        Faroese
       </a>
       , which have a combined total of about 20 million speakers.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">
        East Germanic branch
       </a>
       included
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Burgundians#Language" title="Burgundians">
        Burgundian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Vandalic_language" title="Vandalic language">
        Vandalic
       </a>
       , all of which are now extinct. The last to die off was
       <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">
        Crimean Gothic
       </a>
       , spoken until the late 18th century in some isolated areas of
       <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">
        Crimea
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/SIL_International" title="SIL International">
        SIL
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
         Ethnologue
        </a>
       </i>
       lists 48 different living Germanic languages, 41 of which belong to the Western branch and six to the Northern branch; it places
       <a href="/wiki/Riograndenser_Hunsr%C3%BCckisch_German" title="Riograndenser Hunsrückisch German">
        Riograndenser Hunsrückisch German
       </a>
       in neither of the categories, but it is often considered a German dialect by linguists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The total number of Germanic languages throughout history is unknown as some of them, especially the East Germanic languages, disappeared during or after the
       <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">
        Migration Period
       </a>
       . Some of the West Germanic languages also did not survive past the Migration Period, including
       <a href="/wiki/Lombardic_language" title="Lombardic language">
        Lombardic
       </a>
       . As a result of
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , the German language
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_German_language" title="History of German language">
        suffered
       </a>
       a significant loss of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sprachraum" title="Sprachraum">
         Sprachraum
        </a>
       </i>
       , as well as moribundness and extinction of several of its dialects. In the 21st century, its dialects are dying out anyway
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [nb 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       due to
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">
        Standard German
       </a>
       gaining primacy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The common ancestor of all of the languages in this branch is called
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">
        Proto-Germanic
       </a>
       , also known as Common Germanic, which was spoken in about the middle of the 1st millennium BC in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_Iron_Age_in_Northern_Europe" title="Pre-Roman Iron Age in Northern Europe">
        Iron Age Scandinavia
       </a>
       . Proto-Germanic, along with all of its descendants, is characterised by a number of unique linguistic features, most famously the
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonant
       </a>
       change known as
       <a href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_law" title="Grimm's law">
        Grimm's law
       </a>
       . Early varieties of Germanic entered history with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" title="Germanic tribes">
        Germanic tribes
       </a>
       moving south from
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">
        Scandinavia
       </a>
       in the 2nd century BC, to settle in the area of today's northern Germany and southern Denmark.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
      <div class="toc" id="toc">

          </span>

        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7">
         <a href="#Linguistic_developments">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           4
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Linguistic developments
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_status">
        Modern status
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Modern status">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="West_Germanic_languages">
        West Germanic languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: West Germanic languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       English is an
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        official language
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">
        Belize
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">
        Falkland Islands
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">
        Malta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">
        New Zealand
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">
        Ireland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
        South Africa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">
        Jamaica
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dominica" title="Dominica">
        Dominica
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">
        Guyana
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">
        Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">
        American Samoa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">
        Palau
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Lucia" title="St. Lucia">
        St. Lucia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">
        Grenada
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">
        Barbados
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Vincent_and_the_Grenadines" title="St. Vincent and the Grenadines">
        St. Vincent and the Grenadines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">
        Puerto Rico
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
        Guam
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">
        Hong Kong
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">
        Pakistan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">
        Papua New Guinea
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">
        Vanuatu
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">
        Solomon Islands
       </a>
       and former British colonies in
       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">
        Asia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        Africa
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">
        Oceania
       </a>
       . Furthermore, it is the
       <i>
        de facto
       </i>
       language of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       . It is also a recognized language in
       <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">
        Nicaragua
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
        Malaysia
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">
        American English
       </a>
       -speakers make up the majority of all native Germanic speakers, including also making up the bulk of West Germanic speakers.
      </p>
      <p>
       German is an official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
        Belgium
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">
        Liechtenstein
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">
        Luxembourg
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       and has regional status in
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
        Poland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
        Namibia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
        Denmark
       </a>
       . German also continues to be spoken as a minority language by
       <a href="/wiki/German_diaspora" title="German diaspora">
        immigrant communities
       </a>
       in North America, South America, Central America,
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       and Australia. A German dialect,
       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Dutch" title="Pennsylvania Dutch">
        Pennsylvania Dutch
       </a>
       , is still present amongst
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">
        Anabaptist
       </a>
       populations in
       <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">
        Pennsylvania
       </a>
       in the United States.
      </p>
      <p>
       Dutch is an official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Aruba" title="Aruba">
        Aruba
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
        Belgium
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cura%C3%A7ao" title="Curaçao">
        Curaçao
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sint_Maarten" title="Sint Maarten">
        Sint Maarten
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">
        Suriname
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Netherlands also colonised
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Netherlands_Indies" title="Netherlands Indies">
        Indonesia
       </a>
       , but Dutch was scrapped as an official language after
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesian_independence" title="Indonesian independence">
        Indonesian independence
       </a>
       and today it is only used by older or educated people. Dutch was until 1925 an official language in South Africa but evolved in and was replaced by
       <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        partially mutually intelligible
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Daughter_language" title="Daughter language">
        daughter language
       </a>
       of Dutch.
      </p>
      <p>
       Afrikaans is one of the 11 official languages in South Africa and is a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
       </i>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
        Namibia
       </a>
       . It is used in other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_African" title="Southern African">
        Southern African
       </a>
       nations, as well.
      </p>
      <p>
       Low German (or Low Saxon) is a collection of very diverse dialects spoken in the northeast of the Netherlands and northern Germany.
      </p>
      <p>
       Scots is spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">
        Lowland
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       and parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">
        Ulster
       </a>
       (where the local dialect is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">
        Ulster Scots
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Frisian is spoken among half a million people who live on the southern fringes of the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">
        North Sea
       </a>
       in the Netherlands, Germany, and
       <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
        Denmark
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Luxembourgish is spoken mainly in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Luxembourg" title="Grand Duchy of Luxembourg">
        Grand Duchy of Luxembourg
       </a>
       , but it also extends into small parts of Belgium, France, and Germany.
      </p>
      <p>
       Yiddish, once a native language of some 11 to 13 million people, is used by some Jewish communities throughout the world, mainly in North America, Europe,
       <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
        Israel
       </a>
       , and other regions with
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_population_by_country" title="Jewish population by country">
        Jewish populations
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yivo-yiddish_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-yivo-yiddish-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Limburgish
       <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
        varieties
       </a>
       are spoken in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_of_Limburg_(1815%E2%80%931839)" title="Province of Limburg (1815–1839)">
        Limburg
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">
        Rhineland
       </a>
       regions, along the Dutch–Belgian–German border.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="North_Germanic_languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: North Germanic languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In addition to being the official language in
       <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       is also spoken natively by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finland_Swedes" title="Finland Swedes">
        Swedish-speaking minority
       </a>
       in Finland, which is a large part of the population
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_municipalities_of_Finland_in_which_Finnish_is_not_the_sole_official_language" title="List of municipalities of Finland in which Finnish is not the sole official language">
        along the coast of western and southern
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">
        Finland
       </a>
       . Swedish is also one of the two official languages in Finland, along with
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       , and the only official language in the
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">
        Åland Islands
       </a>
       . Swedish is also spoken by some people in
       <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">
        Estonia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       is an official language of Denmark and in its overseas territory of the
       <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">
        Faroe Islands
       </a>
       , and it is a
       <i>
        lingua franca
       </i>
       and language of education in its other overseas territory of
       <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">
        Greenland
       </a>
       , where it was one of the official languages until 2009. Danish is also spoken natively by the Danish minority in the German state of
       <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">
        Schleswig-Holstein
       </a>
       , where it is recognised as a minority language.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       is the official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">
        Norway
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       is the official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">
        Iceland
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">
        Faroese
       </a>
       is the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">
        Faroe Islands
       </a>
       , and it is also spoken by some people in Denmark.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">
        Statistics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Germanic_tribes_(750BC-1AD).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="537" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Germanic_tribes_%28750BC-1AD%29.png/200px-Germanic_tribes_%28750BC-1AD%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Germanic_tribes_%28750BC-1AD%29.png/300px-Germanic_tribes_%28750BC-1AD%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Germanic_tribes_%28750BC-1AD%29.png/400px-Germanic_tribes_%28750BC-1AD%29.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Germanic_tribes_(750BC-1AD).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The expansion of the Germanic tribes 750 BC – AD 1 (after the
         <i>
          Penguin Atlas of World History
         </i>
         1988):
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #d00; background-color: #f00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Settlements before 750 BC
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #e73; background-color: #f84; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          New settlements by 500 BC
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #da0; background-color: #ff0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          New settlements by 250 BC
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #0d0; background-color: #0f0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          New settlements by AD 1
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_norse,_ca_900.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="1235" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/200px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/300px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/400px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_norse,_ca_900.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The approximate extent of Germanic languages in the early 10th century.:
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <b>
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_West_Norse" title="Old West Norse">
            Old West Norse
           </a>
          </b>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff9933; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <b>
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_East_Norse" title="Old East Norse">
            Old East Norse
           </a>
          </b>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff00ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <b>
           <a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">
            Old Gutnish
           </a>
          </b>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <b>
           <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
            Old English
           </a>
          </b>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
           West Germanic
          </a>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00ff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Continental West Germanic languages (
          <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">
           Old Frisian
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">
           Old Saxon
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">
           Old Dutch
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
           Old High German
          </a>
          ).
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <b>
           <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">
            Crimean Gothic
           </a>
          </b>
          (
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Germanic" title="East Germanic">
           East Germanic
          </a>
          )
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       All Germanic languages are thought to be descended from a hypothetical
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">
        Proto-Germanic
       </a>
       , united by subjection to the sound shifts of
       <a href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_law" title="Grimm's law">
        Grimm's law
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Verner%27s_law" title="Verner's law">
        Verner's law
       </a>
       . These probably took place during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_Iron_Age" title="Pre-Roman Iron Age">
        Pre-Roman Iron Age
       </a>
       of Northern Europe from c. 500 BC.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
        Proto-Germanic
       </a>
       itself was likely spoken after c. 500 BC,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Norse" title="Proto-Norse">
        Proto-Norse
       </a>
       from the 2nd century AD and later is still quite close to reconstructed Proto-Germanic, but other common innovations separating Germanic from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">
        Proto-Indo-European
       </a>
       suggest a common history of pre-Proto-Germanic speakers throughout the
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">
        Nordic Bronze Age
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       From the time of their earliest attestation, the Germanic varieties are divided into three groups:
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">
        East
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North
       </a>
       Germanic. Their exact relation is difficult to determine from the sparse evidence of runic inscriptions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The western group would have formed in the late
       <a href="/wiki/Jastorf_culture" title="Jastorf culture">
        Jastorf culture
       </a>
       , and the eastern group may be derived from the 1st-century
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">
        variety
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">
        Gotland
       </a>
       , leaving southern Sweden as the original location of the northern group. The earliest period of
       <a href="/wiki/Elder_Futhark" title="Elder Futhark">
        Elder Futhark
       </a>
       (2nd to 4th centuries) predates the division in regional script variants, and linguistically essentially still reflect the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_Germanic" title="Common Germanic">
        Common Germanic
       </a>
       stage.
       <a href="/wiki/Vimose_inscriptions" title="Vimose inscriptions">
        Vimose inscriptions
       </a>
       AD 160, are the oldest Germanic writing.
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest coherent Germanic text preserved is the 4th century
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       translation of the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
        New Testament
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">
        Ulfilas
       </a>
       . Early testimonies of West Germanic are in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Frankish" title="Old Frankish">
        Old Frankish
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">
        Old Dutch
       </a>
       (the 5th century
       <a href="/wiki/Bergakker_inscription" title="Bergakker inscription">
        Bergakker inscription
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
        Old High German
       </a>
       (scattered words and sentences 6th century and coherent texts 9th century), and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">
        Old English
       </a>
       (oldest texts 650, coherent texts 10th century). North Germanic is only attested in scattered runic inscriptions, as
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Norse_language" title="Proto-Norse language">
        Proto-Norse
       </a>
       , until it evolves into
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       by about 800.
      </p>
      <p>
       Longer runic inscriptions survive from the 8th and 9th centuries (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eggjum_stone" title="Eggjum stone">
        Eggjum stone
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%B6k_stone" title="Rök stone">
        Rök stone
       </a>
       ), longer texts in the Latin alphabet survive from the 12th century (
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Dslendingab%C3%B3k" title="Íslendingabók">
        Íslendingabók
       </a>
       ), and some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skaldic_poetry" title="Skaldic poetry">
        skaldic poetry
       </a>
       dates back to as early as the 9th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       By about the 10th century, the varieties had diverged enough to make
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        inter-comprehensibility
       </a>
       difficult. The linguistic contact of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">
        Viking
       </a>
       settlers of the
       <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">
        Danelaw
       </a>
       with the
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">
        Anglo-Saxons
       </a>
       left traces in the English language and is suspected to have facilitated the collapse of Old English grammar that resulted in
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
        Middle English
       </a>
       from the 12th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       The East Germanic languages were marginalized from the end of the Migration period. The
       <a href="/wiki/Burgundians" title="Burgundians">
        Burgundians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">
        Goths
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">
        Vandals
       </a>
       became linguistically assimilated by their respective neighbors by about the 7th century, with only
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic_language" title="Crimean Gothic language">
        Crimean Gothic
       </a>
       lingering on until the 18th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the early Middle Ages, the West Germanic languages were separated by the insular development of Middle English on one hand and by the
       <a href="/wiki/High_German_consonant_shift" title="High German consonant shift">
        High German consonant shift
       </a>
       on the continent on the other, resulting in
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">
        Upper German
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">
        Low Saxon
       </a>
       , with graded intermediate
       <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">
        Central German
       </a>
       varieties. By early modern times, the span had extended into considerable differences, ranging from
       <a href="/wiki/Highest_Alemannic_German" title="Highest Alemannic German">
        Highest Alemannic
       </a>
       in the South to
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Low_Saxon" title="Northern Low Saxon">
        Northern Low Saxon
       </a>
       in the North, and, although both extremes are considered German, they are hardly mutually intelligible. The southernmost varieties had completed the second sound shift, while the northern varieties remained unaffected by the consonant shift.
      </p>
      <p>
       The North Germanic languages, on the other hand, remained unified until well past 1000 AD, and in fact the mainland Scandinavian languages still largely retain mutual intelligibility into modern times. The main split in these languages is between the mainland languages and the island languages to the west, especially
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       , which has maintained the grammar of
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       virtually unchanged, while the mainland languages have diverged greatly.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">
        Characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Germanic languages possess a number of defining features compared with other Indo-European languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       Probably the most well-known are the following:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">
         sound changes
        </a>
        known as
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_Law" title="Grimm's Law">
         Grimm's Law
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verner%27s_Law" title="Verner's Law">
         Verner's Law
        </a>
        , which shifted the values of all the Indo-European stop consonants. (For example, original *
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t d dh/
        </span>
        became Germanic *
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /θ t d/
        </span>
        in most cases; compare
        <i>
         three
        </i>
        with
        <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
         Latin
        </a>
        <i>
         tres
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         two
        </i>
        with Latin
        <i>
         duo
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         do
        </i>
        with
        <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">
         Sanskrit
        </a>
        <i>
         dha-
        </i>
        .) The recognition of these two sound laws were seminal events in the understanding of the regular nature of linguistic sound change and the development of the
        <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">
         comparative method
        </a>
        , which forms the basis of modern
        <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">
         historical linguistics
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The development of a strong
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(language)" title="Stress (language)">
         stress
        </a>
        on the first syllable of the word, which triggered significant phonological reduction of all other syllables. This is responsible for the reduction of most of the basic English, Norwegian, Danish and Swedish words into monosyllables, and the common impression of modern English and German as consonant-heavy languages. Examples are Proto-Germanic *strangiþō →
        <i>
         strength
        </i>
        , *aimaitijō → "ant", *haubudan → "head", *hauzijanan → "hear", *harubistaz → German
        <i>
         Herbst
        </i>
        "autumn, harvest",
        <i>
         *hagatusjō
        </i>
        → German
        <i>
         Hexe
        </i>
        "witch, hag".
       </li>
       <li>
        A change known as
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">
         Germanic umlaut
        </a>
        , which modified vowel qualities when a high front vocalic segment (/i/,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iː/
        </span>
        or /j/) followed in the next syllable. Generally, back vowels were fronted, and front vowels were raised. In many languages, the modified vowels are indicated with a
        <a href="/wiki/Diaeresis_(diacritic)" title="Diaeresis (diacritic)">
         diaeresis
        </a>
        (e.g.,
        <i>
         ä ö ü
        </i>
        in German, pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ ø y/
        </span>
        , respectively). This change resulted in pervasive alternations in related words — still extremely prominent in modern German but present only in remnants in modern English (e.g.,
        <i>
         mouse/mice
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         goose/geese
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         broad/breadth
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         tell/told
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         old/elder
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         foul/filth
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         gold/gild
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
         <a href="#cite_note-42">
          [34]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Large numbers of vowel qualities. English is typical in this respect, with around 11–12 vowels in most dialects (not counting diphthongs).
        <a href="/wiki/Standard_Swedish" title="Standard Swedish">
         Standard Swedish
        </a>
        has 17
        <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
         pure vowels (monophthongs)
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
         <a href="#cite_note-43">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
        standard German and Dutch 14, and
        <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
         Danish
        </a>
        at least 11.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The Amstetten dialect of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bavarian_German" title="Bavarian German">
         Bavarian German
        </a>
        has 13 distinctions among long vowels alone, one of the largest such inventories in the world.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">
          Verb second
         </a>
        </i>
        (V2) word order, which is uncommon cross-linguistically. Exactly one noun phrase or adverbial element must precede the verb; in particular, if an adverb or prepositional phrase precedes the verb, then the subject must immediately follow the finite verb. This is no longer present in modern English except in sentences beginning with "Here is," "There is," "Here comes," "There goes," and related expressions, as well as in a few relic sentences such as "Over went the boat", "
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pop_Goes_The_Weasel" title="Pop Goes The Weasel">
         Pop Goes The Weasel
        </a>
        ", the
        <a href="/wiki/Palindrome" title="Palindrome">
         palindrome
        </a>
        "
        <a href="/wiki/Palindrome#Famous_English_palindromes" title="Palindrome">
         Able was I ere I saw Elba
        </a>
        " or "
        <a href="/wiki/Boom_goes_the_dynamite" title="Boom goes the dynamite">
         Boom goes the dynamite
        </a>
        ", and in most if not all (if not an absolute) of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Ws_and_one_H" title="Five Ws and one H">
         Five Ws and one H
        </a>
        questions e.g. "What
        <b>
         has
        </b>
        happened here?", "Who
        <b>
         was
        </b>
        here today?", "Where
        <b>
         will
        </b>
        we go?", "When
        <b>
         did
        </b>
        he go to the stadium?", "Why
        <b>
         would
        </b>
        this happen to us now?", and "How
        <b>
         could
        </b>
        these things get here?", but is found in all other modern Germanic languages.
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Other significant characteristics are:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        The reduction of the various
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">
         tense
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">
         aspect
        </a>
        combinations of the Indo-European verbal system into only two: the
        <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">
         present tense
        </a>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Past_tense" title="Past tense">
         past tense
        </a>
        (also called the
        <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
         preterite
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        A large class of verbs that use a dental
        <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">
         suffix
        </a>
        (/d/ or /t/) instead of
        <a href="/wiki/Apophony" title="Apophony">
         vowel alternation
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_ablaut" title="Indo-European ablaut">
         Indo-European ablaut
        </a>
        ) to indicate past tense. These are called the
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_weak_verb" title="Germanic weak verb">
         Germanic weak verbs
        </a>
        ; the remaining verbs with vowel ablaut are the
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">
         Germanic strong verbs
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        A distinction in
        <a href="/wiki/Definiteness" title="Definiteness">
         definiteness
        </a>
        of a
        <a href="/wiki/Noun_phrase" title="Noun phrase">
         noun phrase
        </a>
        that is marked by different sets of inflectional endings for
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adjectives" title="Adjectives">
         adjectives
        </a>
        , the so-called strong and weak
        <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">
         adjectives
        </a>
        . A similar development happened in the
        <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">
         Balto-Slavic languages
        </a>
        . This distinction has been lost in modern English but was present in
        <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
         Old English
        </a>
        and remains in all other Germanic languages to various degrees.
       </li>
       <li>
        Some words with etymologies that are difficult to link to other Indo-European families but with variants that appear in almost all Germanic languages. See
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_substrate_hypothesis" title="Germanic substrate hypothesis">
         Germanic substrate hypothesis
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Note that some of the above characteristics were not present in Proto-Germanic but developed later as
       <a href="/wiki/Areal_feature" title="Areal feature">
        areal features
       </a>
       that spread from language to language:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Germanic umlaut only affected the
        <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
         North
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
         West Germanic languages
        </a>
        (which represent all modern Germanic languages) but not the now-extinct
        <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">
         East Germanic languages
        </a>
        , such as
        <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
         Gothic
        </a>
        , nor
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
         Proto-Germanic
        </a>
        , the common ancestor of all Germanic languages.
       </li>
       <li>
        The large inventory of vowel qualities is a later development, due to a combination of Germanic umlaut and the tendency in many Germanic languages for pairs of long/short vowels of originally identical quality to develop distinct qualities, with the length distinction sometimes eventually lost. Proto-Germanic had only five distinct vowel qualities, although there were more actual vowel phonemes because length and possibly nasality were phonemic. In modern German, long-short vowel pairs still exist but are also distinct in quality.
       </li>
       <li>
        Proto-Germanic probably had a more general S-O-V-I word order. However, the tendency toward V2 order may have already been present in latent form and may be related to
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wackernagel%27s_Law" title="Wackernagel's Law">
         Wackernagel's Law
        </a>
        , an Indo-European law dictating that sentence
        <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
         clitics
        </a>
        must be placed second.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-46">
          [38]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Roughly speaking, Germanic languages differ in how conservative or how progressive each language is with respect to an overall trend toward
       <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">
        analyticity
       </a>
       . Some, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       and, to a lesser extent, German, have preserved much of the complex
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflectional_morphology" title="Inflectional morphology">
        inflectional morphology
       </a>
       inherited from
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">
        Proto-Germanic
       </a>
       (and in turn from
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
        Proto-Indo-European
       </a>
       ). Others, such as English,
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       , have moved toward a largely analytic type.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_developments">
        Linguistic developments
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Linguistic developments">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The subgroupings of the Germanic languages are defined by shared innovations. It is important to distinguish innovations from cases of linguistic conservatism. That is, if two languages in a family share a characteristic that is not observed in a third language, that is evidence of common ancestry of the two languages
       <i>
        only if
       </i>
       the characteristic is an innovation compared to the family's
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">
        proto-language
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The following innovations are common to the
       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Germanic" title="Northwest Germanic">
        Northwest Germanic
       </a>
       languages (all but
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       ):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The lowering of /u/ to /o/ in initial syllables before /a/ in the following syllable ("a-Umlaut", traditionally called
        <i>
         Brechung
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        "Labial umlaut" in unstressed medial syllables (the conversion of /a/ to /u/ and /ō/ to /ū/ before /m/, or /u/ in the following syllable)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The conversion of /ē
        <sub>
         1
        </sub>
        / into /ā/ (vs. Gothic /ē/) in stressed syllables.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [40]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In unstressed syllables, West Germanic also has this change, but North Germanic has shortened the vowel to /e/, then raised it to /i/. This suggests it was an areal change.
       </li>
       <li>
        The raising of final /ō/ to /u/ (Gothic lowers it to /a/). It is kept distinct from the nasal /ǭ/, which is not raised.
       </li>
       <li>
        The monophthongisation of /ai/ and /au/ to /ē/ and /ō/ in non-initial syllables (however, evidence for the development of /au/ in medial syllables is lacking).
       </li>
       <li>
        The development of an intensified demonstrative ending in /s/ (reflected in English "this" compared to "the")
       </li>
       <li>
        Introduction of a distinct ablaut grade in Class VII
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">
         strong verbs
        </a>
        , while Gothic uses
        <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">
         reduplication
        </a>
        (e.g. Gothic
        <i>
         haihait
        </i>
        ; ON, OE
        <i>
         hēt
        </i>
        , preterite of the Gmc verb
        <i>
         *haitan
        </i>
        "to be called")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        as part of a comprehensive reformation of the Gmc Class VII from a reduplicating to a new ablaut pattern, which presumably started in verbs beginning with vowel or /h/
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (a development which continues the general trend of de-reduplication in Gmc
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ); there are forms (such as OE dial.
        <i>
         heht
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         hēt
        </i>
        ) which retain traces of reduplication even in West and North Germanic
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The following innovations are also common to the
       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Germanic" title="Northwest Germanic">
        Northwest Germanic
       </a>
       languages but represent
       <a href="/wiki/Areal_feature" title="Areal feature">
        areal changes
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Proto-Germanic /z/ &gt; /r/ (e.g. Gothic
        <i>
         dius
        </i>
        ; ON
        <i>
         dȳr
        </i>
        , OHG
        <i>
         tior
        </i>
        , OE
        <i>
         dēor
        </i>
        , "wild animal"); note that this is not present in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Norse" title="Proto-Norse">
         Proto-Norse
        </a>
        and must be ordered after
        <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
         West Germanic
        </a>
        loss of final /z/
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">
         Germanic umlaut
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The following innovations are common to the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic languages
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Loss of final /z/. In single-syllable words, Old High German retains it (as /r/), while it disappears in the other West Germanic languages.
       </li>
       <li>
        Change of [ð] (fricative allophone of /d/) to stop [d] in all environments.
       </li>
       <li>
        Change of /lþ/ to stop /ld/ (except word-finally).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_gemination" title="West Germanic gemination">
         West Germanic gemination
        </a>
        of consonants, except
        <i>
         r
        </i>
        , before /j/. This only occurred in short-stemmed words due to
        <a href="/wiki/Sievers%27_law" title="Sievers' law">
         Sievers' law
        </a>
        . Gemination of /p/, /t/, /k/ and /h/ is also observed before liquids.
       </li>
       <li>
        Labiovelar consonants become plain velar when non-initial.
       </li>
       <li>
        A particular type of
        <i>
         umlaut
        </i>
        /e-u-i/ &gt; /i-u-i/.
       </li>
       <li>
        The change of /b/ or /g/ to /w/ before nasal consonant.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Changes to the 2nd person singular past-tense: Replacement of the past-singular stem vowel with the past-plural stem vowel, and substitution of the ending
        <i>
         -t
        </i>
        with
        <i>
         -ī
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Short forms (
        <i>
         *stān, stēn
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         *gān, gēn
        </i>
        ) of the verbs for "stand" and "go"; but note that
        <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">
         Crimean Gothic
        </a>
        also has
        <i>
         gēn
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The development of a
        <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">
         gerund
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The following innovations are common to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic" title="Ingvaeonic">
        Ingvaeonic
       </a>
       subgroup of the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic languages
       </a>
       , which includes English, Frisian, and in a few cases Dutch and Low German, but not High German:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The so-called
        <a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_nasal_spirant_law" title="Ingvaeonic nasal spirant law">
         Ingvaeonic nasal spirant law
        </a>
        , with loss of /n/ before voiceless fricatives: e.g.
        <i>
         *munþ
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         *gans
        </i>
        &gt; Old English
        <i>
         mūþ, gōs
        </i>
        &gt; "mouth, goose", but German
        <i>
         Mund, Gans
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The loss of the Germanic
        <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_pronoun" title="Reflexive pronoun">
         reflexive pronoun
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The reduction of the three Germanic
        <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">
         verbal
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">
         plural
        </a>
        forms into one form ending in
        <i>
         -þ
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The development of Class III weak verbs into a relic class consisting of four verbs (
        <i>
         *sagjan
        </i>
        "to say",
        <i>
         *hugjan
        </i>
        "to think",
        <i>
         *habjan
        </i>
        "to have",
        <i>
         *libjan
        </i>
        "to live"; cf. the numerous Old High German verbs in
        <i>
         -ēn
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        The split of the Class II weak verb ending
        <i>
         *-ō-
        </i>
        into
        <i>
         *-ō-/-ōja-
        </i>
        (cf. Old English
        <i>
         -ian
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         -ōjan
        </i>
        , but Old High German
        <i>
         -ōn
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Development of a plural ending
        <b>
         *-ōs
        </b>
        in a-stem nouns (note, Gothic also has
        <i>
         -ōs
        </i>
        , but this is an independent development, caused by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_devoicing" title="Terminal devoicing">
         terminal devoicing
        </a>
        of
        <i>
         *-ōz
        </i>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">
         Old Frisian
        </a>
        has
        <i>
         -ar
        </i>
        , which is thought to be a late borrowing from
        <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
         Danish
        </a>
        ). Cf. modern English plural
        <i>
         -(e)s
        </i>
        , but German plural
        <i>
         -e
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Possibly, the
        <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
         monophthongization
        </a>
        of Germanic
        <i>
         *ai
        </i>
        to
        <i>
         ē/ā
        </i>
        (this may represent independent changes in Old Saxon and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian" title="Anglo-Frisian">
         Anglo-Frisian
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The following innovations are common to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian" title="Anglo-Frisian">
        Anglo-Frisian
       </a>
       subgroup of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" title="Ingvaeonic languages">
        Ingvaeonic languages
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Raising of nasalized
        <i>
         a, ā
        </i>
        into
        <i>
         o, ō
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_brightening" title="Anglo-Frisian brightening">
         Anglo-Frisian brightening
        </a>
        : Fronting of non-nasal
        <i>
         a, ā
        </i>
        to
        <i>
         æ,ǣ
        </i>
        when not followed by
        <i>
         n
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Metathesis_(linguistics)" title="Metathesis (linguistics)">
         Metathesis
        </a>
        of
        <i>
         CrV
        </i>
        into
        <i>
         CVr
        </i>
        , where
        <i>
         C
        </i>
        represents any consonant and
        <i>
         V
        </i>
        any vowel.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Monophthongization" title="Monophthongization">
         Monophthongization
        </a>
        of
        <i>
         ai
        </i>
        into
        <i>
         ā
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Common_linguistic_features">
        Common linguistic features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Common linguistic features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The oldest Germanic languages all share a number of features, which are assumed to be inherited from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
        Proto-Germanic
       </a>
       . Phonologically, it includes the important sound changes known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_Law" title="Grimm's Law">
        Grimm's Law
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verner%27s_Law" title="Verner's Law">
        Verner's Law
       </a>
       , which introduced a large number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">
        fricatives
       </a>
       ; late
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">
        Proto-Indo-European
       </a>
       had only one, /s/.
      </p>
      <p>
       The main vowel developments are the merging (in most circumstances) of long and short /a/ and /o/, producing short /a/ and long /ō/. That likewise affected the
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       , with PIE /ai/ and /oi/ merging into /ai/ and PIE /au/ and /ou/ merging into /au/. PIE /ei/ developed into long /ī/. PIE long /ē/ developed into a vowel denoted as /ē
       <sub>
        1
       </sub>
       / (often assumed to be phonetically
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ǣ]
       </span>
       ), while a new, fairly uncommon long vowel /ē
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       / developed in varied and not completely understood circumstances. Proto-Germanic had no
       <a href="/wiki/Front_rounded_vowel" title="Front rounded vowel">
        front rounded vowels
       </a>
       , but all Germanic languages except for
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       subsequently developed them through the process of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-umlaut" title="I-umlaut">
        i-umlaut
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Proto-Germanic developed a strong stress accent on the first syllable of the root, but remnants of the original free PIE accent are visible due to Verner's Law, which was sensitive to this accent). That caused a steady erosion of vowels in unstressed syllables. In Proto-Germanic, that had progressed only to the point that absolutely-final short vowels (other than /i/ and /u/) were lost and absolutely-final long vowels were shortened, but all of the early literary languages show a more advanced state of vowel loss. This ultimately resulted in some languages (like Modern English) losing practically all vowels following the main stress and the consequent rise of a very large number of monosyllabic words.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Table_of_outcomes">
        Table of outcomes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Table of outcomes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The following table shows the main outcomes of Proto-Germanic vowels and consonants in the various older languages. For vowels, only the outcomes in stressed syllables are shown. Outcomes in unstressed syllables are quite different, vary from language to language and depend on a number of other factors (such as whether the syllable was medial or final, whether the syllable was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_syllable" title="Open syllable">
        open
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_syllable" title="Closed syllable">
        closed
       </a>
       and (in some cases) whether the preceding syllable was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_syllable" title="Light syllable">
        light
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_syllable" title="Heavy syllable">
        heavy
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Notes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         C-
        </i>
        means before a vowel (word-initially, or sometimes after a consonant).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         -C-
        </i>
        means between vowels.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         -C
        </i>
        means after a vowel (word-finally or before a consonant). Word-final outcomes generally occurred
        <i>
         after
        </i>
        deletion of final short vowels, which occurred shortly after Proto-Germanic and is reflected in the history of all written languages except for
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Norse" title="Proto-Norse">
         Proto-Norse
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The above three are given in the order
        <i>
         C-
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         -C-
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         -C
        </i>
        . If one is omitted, the previous one applies. For example,
        <i>
         f, -[v]-
        </i>
        means that
        <i>
         [v]
        </i>
        occurs after a vowel regardless of what follows.
       </li>
       <li>
        Something like
        <i>
         a(…u)
        </i>
        means "
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        if /u/ occurs in the next syllable".
       </li>
       <li>
        Something like
        <i>
         a(n)
        </i>
        means "
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        if /n/ immediately follows".
       </li>
       <li>
        Something like
        <i>
         (n)a
        </i>
        means "
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        if /n/ immediately precedes".
       </li>
      </ul>
